Kirsch v. Prince George's County


No. 111, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


331 Md. 89; 626 A.2d 372; 1993 Md. LEXIS 94



June 25, 1993  
June 25, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:   [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Larnzell Martin, Jr., JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY FOR THE ENTRY OF A JUDGMENT DECLARING ZONING ORDINANCE BILL NUMBER CB-152-1989 INVALID; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, property owners and students, filed an action against appellee county seeking declaratory relief from Prince George's County Zoning Ordinance Bill No. CB-152-1989 (1989), which regulated the rental of residential property to three or more students. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the trial court's grant of the summary judgment in favor of the county, and the property owners and students appealed.


OVERVIEW: The issue before the court was whether CB-152-1989, the "mini-dorm" ordinance, deprived the students of equal protection of the law by discriminating against a class of persons in violation of the Fourteenth Amendment of the United States Constitution and Article 24 of the Maryland Declaration of Rights. The court held that: (1) to withstand equal protection scrutiny, the ordinance should have been rationally related to a legitimate governmental purpose; (2) the ordinance's differentiation between permissible residential tenant classes by creating more strenuous zoning requirements for some and less for others based solely on the occupation that the tenant pursued away from the residence was an arbitrary classification that was forbidden under Article 24; and (3) the zoning classification of residential property was wholly unrelated to the stated purpose of the ordinance, and its impact upon persons who were registered as full-time or part-time students at an institution of higher learning denied those students equal protection of the laws under the Fourteenth Amendment and Article 24 of the Maryland Declaration of Rights.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment that affirmed the grant of summary judgment in favor of the county. The case was remanded with instructions to remand the case to the trial court for entry of a judgment declaring the "mini-dorm" zoning ordinance invalid.

Liberty Nursing Ctr., Inc. v. Department of Health & Mental Hygiene


No. 70, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


330 Md. 433; 624 A.2d 941; 1993 Md. LEXIS 76



May 17, 1993  
May 17, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. (Circuit Court for Baltimore City). David P. Mitchell, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY FOR FURTHER REMAND TO THE NURSING HOME REVIEW BOARD FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS IN THIS COURT AND THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY THE RESPONDENT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant nursing home sought judicial review pursuant to Md. Code Ann., Health-Gen. § 15-108(f) (1992), of a decision by respondent state agency to disallow interest on a loan as a reimbursable expense. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the trial court's determination, which upheld the state agency. The court granted the nursing home's petition for certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The nursing home sought to be reimbursed for an interest expense paid to an unrelated organization. The lower courts affirmed the state agency decision that the interest expense was not reimbursable. On appeal the court held that: (1) 42 C.F.R. § 413.153(a) provided that interest on current and capital indebtedness was an allowable cost, (2) the lender and the nursing home were not related, (3) the fact that a principal of the nursing home purchased the nursing home facilities for a specific purpose or to achieve a specific result was not the dispositive question; the question was whether the purchase was necessary from the nursing home's perspective, (4) nothing in 42 C.F.R. § 413.153, or the policy underlying it, prohibited allowing the reimbursement if it was done for a reason related to patient care, which was a determination that had not been made in the case.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment with directions that the case be remanded for further determinations at the administrative level in accordance with the views expressed in the opinion.

Maryland State Police v. Warwick Supply & Equip. Co.


No. 117, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


330 Md. 474; 624 A.2d 1238; 1993 Md. LEXIS 68



May 10, 1993  
May 10, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected May 18, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. John C. Themelis, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO AFFIRM THE ORDER OF THE MARYLAND STATE BOARD OF CONTRACT APPEALS; COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLEE. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The court issued a writ of certiorari to review the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which reversed an administrative judgment in favor of appellants, Maryland Department of General Services and Maryland State Police, dismissing appellee contractor's appeal as time barred, and remanded for a hearing on the merits, in a claim for money owed under a contract to replace concrete sidewalks at the state police headquarters.


OVERVIEW: The contractor appealed to the Maryland State Board of Contract Appeals (MSBCA) alleging appellants owed it additional money. The MSBCA granted appellants' motion to dismiss the appeal as untimely. The trial court reversed the judgment, ruling that Md. Regs. Code tit. 21, § 10.04.06B was overbroad and violated the separation of powers doctrine, and finding that the contractor's request for an equitable adjustment was never denied. The court granted certiorari. On review, the court reversed the trial court. The court held that the state legislature expressly authorized appeals by contractors to the MSBCA when a state agency failed to reach a decision on a claim within 180 days of the claim. Md. Code Ann. State. Fin. & Proc. § 15-220 required a contractor to appeal to the MSBCA within 30 days of the statutory expiration period. The challenged regulations did not violate the separation of powers doctrine as they only mirrored the statute. The trial court exceeded its scope of review by finding that the request for equitable adjustment remained under active consideration. Finally, the MSBCA's findings were supported by substantial evidence and were not arbitrary or capricious.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment and remanded to the trial court with instructions to affirm the order of the administrative agency. The court held that the action was time-barred, applicable regulations did not violate the separation of powers doctrine because they merely mirrored statutory provisions and that the agency's factual findings were supported by substantial evidence, and were not arbitrary or capricious.

Tyler v. State


No. 87, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


330 Md. 261; 623 A.2d 648; 1993 Md. LEXIS 57



April 26, 1993  
April 26, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Ernest Loveless, Jr., JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASES REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTION TO REMAND TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY WITH DIRECTION TO VACATE ITS JUDGMENTS AND GRANT TYLER AND EILAND A NEW TRIAL. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendants appealed the decision from the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirming the trial court's judgment that had convicted defendants of murder.


OVERVIEW: After a jury was impaneled in defendants' joint trial for murder, defendants objected to the panel due to the state's use of its preemptory challenges to exclude jurors on the basis of gender and race. The trial court limited the challenge to racial discrimination and denied the challenges based on race. The appellate court affirmed defendants' convictions. On appeal, the court reversed the judgment of the appellate court. The court held that pursuant to Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 46, which mandated that gender was a suspect classification subject to strict scrutiny, and Md. Const. Decl. Rts. art. 24, which guaranteed equal protection, the state was prevented from using peremptory challenges to exclude potential jurors based on their gender. Moreover, because the record made clear that the state excluded jurors based on gender, the court reversed the cause without giving the state the opportunity to explain its peremptory challenges. The court remanded the cause to the appellate court with instructions to remand to the trial court with instructions to vacate its judgments and grant defendants a new trial.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the appellate court, which had affirmed defendants' convictions for murder, and remanded the cases to the appellate court with instructions to remand to the trial court with instructions to vacate its judgments and grant defendants a new trial.

Anderson v. Department of Pub. Safety & Correctional Servs.


No. 115, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


330 Md. 187; 623 A.2d 198; 1993 Md. LEXIS 58



April 23, 1993  
April 23, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to cert. to Court of Special Appeals. Robert I. H. Hammerman, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY VACATED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTION TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE DEPARTMENT OF PERSONNEL WITH DIRECTION TO VACATE ITS ORDER REMOVING WILLIAM HENRY ANDERSON, JR. FROM STATE SERVICE AND TO CONDUCT FURTHER PROCEEDINGS IN ACCORDANCE WITH THIS OPINION; COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLEES. 


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant state employee was suspended from his position as a correction officer with appellee Department of Correction. The administrative law judge (ALJ) proposed that the state employee be reinstated. The Department of Personnel (DOP) designee renounced the decision of the ALJ and ordered that the state employee be separated from state service. The Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) affirmed and the state employee sought review.


OVERVIEW: The state employee was a correction officer that was involved in a scuffle with a prisoner. The state employee requested a hearing after he was suspended without pay. The DOP delegated to the Office of Administrative Hearings (OAH) the authority to conduct a hearing on the charges and to propose a decision. The ALJ of the OAH conducted extensive hearings in which the transcript contained 551 pages. The ALJ proposed that the state employee be reinstated. The DOP designee renounced the proposed decision of the ALJ and ordered that the state employee be separated from state service. The order was affirmed by the circuit court. On appeal, the court held that: (1) credibility of the witnesses was of the utmost importance; (2) the DOP designee did not give proper deference to the ALJ's assessment of the credibility of the witnesses; (3) there was not substantial evidence to support the order of the DOP; and (4) the DOP designee must reconsider her order in the light of the interrelation between the function of the DOP designee and the AJL.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the circuit court's order and remanded the cause to the circuit court with directions to remand the case to the DOP with directions to vacate its order that removed the state employee from state service. The court ordered the DOP to conduct further proceeding in accordance with the opinion.

Patuxent Inst. Bd. of Review v. Hancock


No. 5 September Term, 1992


Court of Appeals of Maryland


329 Md. 556; 620 A.2d 917; 1993 Md. LEXIS 36



March 12, 1993  
March 12, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected April 14, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. (Circuit Court for Howard County). Cornelius F. Sybert, Jr. JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED, EXCEPT THAT THE RESPONDENT SHALL BE RELEASED PURSUANT TO THE TERMS OF THE AUGUST 8, 1990 ORDER OF PAROLE. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The defendant was granted parole by respondent Institution Board of Review (board) and was simultaneously served with a notice of a hearing to determine whether probable cause existed to believe he had violated parole. At the hearing the defendant was ordered held pending a formal parole revocation hearing. The trial court affirmed and the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) reversed and ordered the defendant released. The board sought review.


OVERVIEW: The defendant was incarcerated after he was convicted of murder, attempted murder, and assault and battery. Md. Ann. Code art. 31B, §11(b)(2) (1957, 1983 Repl. Vol.), then in effect, required that the governor approve parole for an inmate serving a life sentence. The governor rejected the recommendation. Section 11(b)(2) was held not to be applied retroactively and a circuit court granted the defendant's petition for habeas corpus relief. The board did not appeal. The board issued respondent an order of parole and simultaneously served the defendant with notice of a preliminary hearing to determine whether the defendant had violated the conditions of his parole by not participating in treatment and counseling sessions. The defendant was ordered held pending a formal hearing. The trial court affirmed the board's order, but the special court of appeals ordered that the defendant be released. On appeal, the court held that (1) the defendant's parole could not be revoked for conditions of parole he was not aware of until the time of parole, and (2) the board could rescind the defendant's parole for misconduct after parole and before physical release.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the court of special appeals and ordered the defendant paroled in accordance with the board's order of parole. The court also ruled that before the defendant was released, the board was entitled to conduct an investigation of the institutional records to determine whether the defendant had engaged in misconduct that justified revocation of his parole.

Talbot County v. Skipper


No. 164, September Term, 1990


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


329 Md. 481; 620 A.2d 880; 1993 Md. LEXIS 31



March 10, 1993, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Talbot County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. Edward D. E. Rollins, Jr., JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiffs, farmers and fertilizer company, filed a petition for a writ of certiorari, which the court granted. Defendant county appealed the decision of the Circuit Court for Talbot County (Maryland), which held that Talbot County, Md., Code § 19-8(j) was preempted by state law. The farmers and fertilizer company filed a complaint for declaratory and injunctive relief against the county.


OVERVIEW: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court. The court held that Md. Code Ann., Envir. tit. 9(2), pt. III, preempted a Talbot County ordinance that required a land owner to record certain information in the county land records before applying sewage sludge to his land in accordance with a state permit. The county conceded that Talbot County, Md., Code §§ 19-8(j)(ii) and (j)(iii) conflicted with state law and were thereby preempted. The court found a general legislative purpose to create an all-encompassing state scheme of sewage sludge regulation. The comprehensiveness of the scheme indicated the legislative intent to preempt the entire field of law from local regulation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the lower court, which had held that a county ordinance requiring a permit for sewage sludge application was preempted by state law.

Powers v. State


No. 91, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


329 Md. 321; 619 A.2d 538; 1993 Md. LEXIS 18

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR A NEW TRIAL ON THE CRIMINAL INFORMATION CHARGING THE FOUR VIOLATIONS OF THE CONTROLLED DANGEROUS SUBSTANCES LAWS. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY THE MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Following a nonjury trial, the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) found defendant guilty of loitering, in violation of Baltimore City Code art. 19, § 58C, possession of cocaine, and possession of heroin. Defendant sought review of his conviction.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was arrested for loitering in a certified drug free zone, in violation of art. 19, § 58. The arresting officer searched defendant incident to the arrest and found several bags of cocaine and heroin. Defendant was charged with violating art. 19, § 58, and possession of cocaine and heroin. Defendant filed a motion to dismiss the art. 19, § 58 charge, claiming that the ordinance was unconstitutional. He also filed a motion to suppress the seized contraband, arguing that it was the fruit of an illegal arrest. The trial court denied the motions and found defendant guilty as charged. The court reversed defendant's conviction under art. 19, § 58, concluding that there was insufficient evidence to establish that defendant was loitering in a certified drug free zone for the purpose of engaging in prohibited drug-related activity. The court also found that defendant's arrest was illegal because the officer did not ask defendant to leave the area prior to arresting him, as required under art. 19, § 58C(d). Accordingly, the court reversed defendant's possession convictions because the contraband seized by the officer was the fruit of an illegal arrest.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's convictions for loitering, possession of cocaine, and possession of heroin, and remanded the matter to the trial court for further proceedings.

Dawson v. State


No. 49, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


329 Md. 275; 619 A.2d 111; 1993 Md. LEXIS 14



February 8, 1993, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY: As Corrected March 22, 1993.


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Harford County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. Stephen M. Waldron, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HARFORD COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY APPELLANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court for Harford County (Maryland), which convicted him under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 286D (1957, 1992 repl. vol.) for distributing a controlled dangerous substance within 1,000 feet of school property.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of distributing a controlled substance, cocaine, within 1,000 feet of school property. Defendant appealed the conviction arguing insufficient evidence and that because school was not in session, the conviction under the statute was an offense to due process. The court affirmed the conviction, holding that there was sufficient evidence, in the form of police testimony, for a jury to determine that defendant was the person who sold the drugs. The court found that the hours that children frequented the areas surrounding school grounds were not limited to those in which the school was open for classroom instruction. School grounds served as a location for a variety of school-sponsored extracurricular activities as well as community-sponsored sports and social activities involving children which may take place during evening and weekend hours. The court held that § 286D was designed to deter illicit dealers from conducting their operations near school property regardless of whether school was in session.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed defendant's conviction for the distribution of a controlled substance near school property.

State v. Prince Georgians for Glendening

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff organization filed a declaratory judgment action against defendant state and sought a declaration that a particular statute was unconstitutional. The Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) entered judgment in favor of the organization. The state appealed.


OVERVIEW: The state legislature passed a statute that would regulate political contributions from landowners and developers to certain elected officials. The organization contended that the combination of different subject matters in the same bill violated the mandate of Md. Const. art. The lower court granted the organization's motion for summary judgment and declared that the act in question did not satisfy that constitutional mandate. The appellate court held that the two subjects were "distinct and incongruous" and "distinct and separate." The court determined that the ethics provisions were severable from the act, leaving the tax provisions fully effective.


OUTCOME: The trial court's summary judgment in favor of the organization in their declaratory judgment action regarding the propriety of certain legislation was affirmed.

Williams v. State


No. 7, September Term, 1992


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


329 Md. 1; 616 A.2d 1275; 1992 Md. LEXIS 197



December 23, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Wicomico County). D. William Simpson JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AS TO COUNT 1 OF THE INDICTMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR WICOMICO COUNTY WITH DIRECTIONS TO VACATE THE PETITIONER'S CONVICTION FOR BEING A "DRUG KINGPIN" UNDER COUNT 1 OF THE INDICTMENT. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY WICOMICO COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed a decision of the Maryland Court of Special Appeals, which affirmed his conviction and sentence under Maryland's Drug Kingpin Act (Kingpin Act), Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 286 (1992 Repl. Vol.), as well as for various conspiracy counts.


OVERVIEW: As a result of an undercover narcotics investigation targeting defendant's brother, defendant was indicted and convicted under the Kingpin Act for his involvement in a conspiracy to distribute 448 grams of cocaine. Defendant challenged his conviction alleging that § 286 was unconstitutional and that the evidence was insufficient to sustain his kingpin conviction. The appellate court affirmed the conviction, and the court reversed as to the kingpin conviction. The court concluded that the Kingpin Act was not unconstitutionally vague where the statute was not a trap for the unwary and where the meaning of the statute was fairly ascertainable. The court further found that the statute was not vague as applied to the facts of the present case. However, the court held that the trial court erred in submitting the kingpin count to the jury because of insufficient evidence. It was clear that § 286 was intended to apply to a leader of a drug trafficking network and not to the defendant whose role was basically as a courier of the drugs. The court found that the evidence supported the fact that defendant's brother was the sole kingpin in the criminal episode.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the appellate court's affirmance of defendant's conviction for being a drug kingpin, as charged under one count of the indictment. The court remanded for the trial court to vacate the kingpin conviction. The court affirmed the conspiracy convictions.

Bruce v. State


No. 133, September Term, 1991


COURT OF APPEALS OR MARYLAND


328 Md. 594; 616 A.2d 392; 1992 Md. LEXIS 188



December 10, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Review of death penalty sentence (Circuit Court for Prince George's County). Audrey E. Melbourne, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed the judgment of the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which sentenced defendant to death upon his convictions for five counts of murder, six counts of use of a handgun in the commission of a felony, one count of attempted murder, one count of assault with intent to murder, one count of conspiracy to rob, and one count of conspiracy to commit murder.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of five counts of murder, six counts of use of a handgun in the commission of a felony, one count of attempted murder, one count of assault with intent to murder, one count of conspiracy to rob, and one count of conspiracy to commit murder. At the sentencing proceeding, the jury returned a death sentence for each of two murders, and the trial court sentenced defendant for the remaining convictions. The court affirmed the convictions but vacated the death sentence and remanded for a new sentencing proceeding. The new jury again sentenced defendant to death, and the court affirmed. The court found that the trial judge was not required make an on-the-record determination that defendant was knowingly and voluntarily proceeding with a jury sentencing. The trial judge acted properly in denying defendant's request to trifurcate his sentencing proceeding, because the principalship and other sentencing-related issues were to be resolved in a unitary sentencing proceeding. There was no plain error in the instructions that were given to the jury. There was evidence to support the finding of an aggravating circumstance outweighing the mitigating circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment sentencing defendant to death for his convictions for murder, use of a handgun in the commission of a felony, attempted murder, assault with intent to murder, conspiracy to rob, and conspiracy to commit murder.

Hartless v. State


No. 116, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


327 Md. 558; 611 A.2d 581; 1992 Md. LEXIS 144



August 26, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. (Circuit Court for Howard County). J. Thomas Nissel, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED, WITH COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: After defendant disclosed an intention to present expert psychiatric testimony as to the absence of specific intent to kill, plaintiff state sought and was granted a mental examination. However, no psychiatric testimony was ultimately admitted at trial. Defendant was convicted in the Court of Special Appeals, Circuit Court for Howard County (Maryland), on charges including first degree premeditated murder and thereafter sought certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The trial court, while requiring defendant to be examined by the state's mental health professionals, forbade the disclosure of results to the state until after the trial began and limited the state's use thereof to rebuttal of any defense psychiatric testimony. Because defendant's psychiatric testimony was then excluded, the state never offered any psychiatric testimony. After conviction, defendant claimed error in the rulings allowing the examination and excluding his expert testimony. The court affirmed. It found that Md. R. Crim. Causes 4-263(d)(1) authorized the lower court to force defendant to submit to examination, that no constitutional violation occurred, and that the lower court did not abuse its discretion in ordering the examination and limiting access to and use of the results. The court also found no abuse of discretion in the exclusion of defendant's psychiatric testimony. The court held that the evidence was inadmissible to show defendant's actual intent at the time of the offense because it was incompetent. It also was inadmissible as a "psychological profile" because it lacked an adequate factual basis and bore no relevance to issues of premeditation or intent.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the lower court's rulings.

Bd. of Supervisors of Elections v. Smallwood


Nos. 71, 72, September Term, 1990


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


327 Md. 220; 608 A.2d 1222; 1992 Md. LEXIS 124



July 17, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected July 22, 1992. Second Correction August 17, 1992. Reported at 327 Md. 220, at 228.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. Bruce C. Williams, JUDGE for No. 71.

Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. John F. Fader, II, JUDGE, for No. 72.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland) and the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland) held that ballot initiatives to place proposed "property tax limitation" charter amendments on the ballot in both counties, and a "ballot initiative amendment" on the ballot in Anne Arundel county, were unconstitutional in violation of Md. Const. art. XI-A, § 5. Appellants, voters in both counties, appealed from the rulings.


OVERVIEW: The court held that because the voters of a charter county could not reserve to themselves the power to initiate legislation, since such initiative conflicted with the terms of Md. Const. art. XI-A, § 3, the proposed "ballot initiative" amendment was unconstitutional. However, the court found that the proposed "property tax limitation" charter amendments were facially valid and did not conflict with public general law. Those amendments would not have divested the county councils of the ability to set the property tax rates, but merely would have prohibited collection above a specified cap. The court found that the "roll back" provision and "escape clause" provisions of the amendments, although invalid, were severable from the valid portions of the "property tax limitation" amendments. Thus, the "property tax limitation" proposed amendments, following the severance of the invalid provisions, was properly submitted to the voters of Baltimore and Ann Arundel counties.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed that portion of the judgment prohibiting the placement of the proposed "ballot initiative amendment" on the ballot. The court reversed the judgments on the "property tax limitation" charter amendments after the court severed the invalid roll back and escape clause provisions.

Booth v. State


No. 20, September Term, 1991


COURT OF APPEALS OF MARYLAND


327 Md. 142; 608 A.2d 162; 1992 Md. LEXIS 119



June 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City. Edward J. Angeletti, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant sought review of the judgment from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland) that found him guilty of murder and imposed the death sentence pursuant to Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413.


OVERVIEW: Appellant was found guilty of murder and appealed from the death sentence which a jury, unanimously and for the third time, imposed on him as the direct perpetrator of the murder. Appellant claimed the trial court erred by giving a modified Allen charge after the jury reported an inability to agree on his first-degree principalship, and by denying bifurcation of the sentencing proceeding. The court affirmed. The court found that the sentence of death was not imposed under the influence of passion, prejudice, or any other arbitrary factor. The trial judge did not have discretion to bifurcate the sentencing proceeding in order to separate out the principalship issue under Md. R. Crim. Causes 4-343. Unanimously, and beyond a reasonable doubt, the jury found defendant was a principal in the first degree to the murder. Unanimously, and by a preponderance of the evidence, the jury found that the seven statutorily recognized mitigating factors did not exist and that there were no other mitigating factors. The death sentence as applied under Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 413 was proper.


OUTCOME: The judgment was affirmed.

Granahan v. Prince George's County


No. 68, September Term, 1991


Court of Appeals of Maryland


326 Md. 346; 605 A.2d 91; 1992 Md. LEXIS 63



April 24, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Circuit Court for Prince George's County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. Robert J. Woods, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED; COSTS TO BE PAID BY PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of the decision from the Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland), which held that appellee county had the authority to levy a necessary tax for the payment of bonds issued prior to the passage of a tax rate limitation.


OVERVIEW: The county provided for a real property tax rate in excess of $ 2.40 per $ 100 of assessed value, a limitation imposed in 1984 upon the amount of real property taxes collected. In a declaratory judgment and injunction action against the county, the taxpayer contended that the real property tax rate limitation restricted the county's authority to exceed the limitation in order to service the debt incurred on its general obligation bonds issued prior to the enactment of the limitation. The circuit court held that the county had the authority to levy the tax. The court granted a petition for certiorari and affirmed the circuit court's decision. The court held that: (1) where the 1984 limitation did not clearly manifest a retroactive intention, to afford retroactive effect would interfere with substantive rights and thus the county's levy in excess of $ 2.40 in order to service pre-limitation debt was appropriate; and (2) where the trial court carefully considered all of the circumstances of a second taxpayer's motion to intervene bearing in mind that the suit was a collusive one, the court found no reason to disturb the circuit court's exercise of discretion in denying the motion.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the circuit court's decision, which held that the county had the authority to levy a real property tax in excess of a rate limitation imposed by a charter amendment in order to service debt incurred on general obligation bonds issued prior to the enactment of the limitation.

The supervisor of assessments attempted to foreclose on the church's land for nonpayment of taxes. The county sold the property at a tax sale to respondent purchaser. The court held that the failure to mail a copy of the order of publication to the church at the correct address resulted in the court not acquiring jurisdiction, and necessitated vacating the order foreclosing the right of redemption.

Ball v. United Parcel Service, Inc.


Misc. No. 11, September Term, 1991


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 652; 602 A.2d 1176; 1992 Md. LEXIS 40; 7 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 394; 121 Lab. Cas. (CCH) P56,878



March 11, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected March 17, 1992. Second Correction March 18, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certified question from the U.S. District Court of Maryland. Herbert F. Murray, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: CERTIFIED QUESTION ANSWERED AS HEREIN SET FORTH; COSTS TO BE EQUALLY DIVIDED BETWEEN THE PARTIES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: As it related to plaintiff former employee's wrongful termination action against defendant employer, the United States District Court for the District of Maryland certified a question as to whether a charitable fund-raising organization was a social or economic association within the meaning of criminal statute, Md. Ann. Code art. 27, § 562A(a) (1987).


OVERVIEW: The employee alleged in her wrongful termination action that her employer fired her for refusing to give time and money to a certain fund-raising organization. She further alleged that the policy violated public policy, as set forth in § 562A. The trial court certified the question as to whether the organization was included as a social or economic association within the meaning of § 562A, and the court held that the statute did not include charitable organizations. The court looked to the rules of statutory construction, determining that a penal statute was to be strictly construed. The court found that the non-profit fund-raising entity was not a social organization within the meaning of the statute because its predominant purpose was not social activities. Further, because the legislature gave a specific definition to a charitable organization, under Md. Ann. Code art. 41, § 3-201(b)(1) (1990), it intended such an organization to be different than a social or economic organization. The court found it totally unpersuasive that the legislature intended to include all fund-raising charitable organizations in its proscription under § 562A.


OUTCOME: The court, in answer to a certified question, determined that a charitable fund-raising organization was not a social or economic association or organization within the proscription of a Maryland criminal statute.

Hawkins v. Department of Public Safety & Correctional Services


No. 63, September Term, 1991


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 621; 602 A.2d 712; 1992 Md. LEXIS 41; 7 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 382



March 10, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. John C. Themelis, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE APPELLANT, DONALD H. HAWKINS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee guard instituted appeals contending that appellant department infringed upon his First Amendment rights to free speech in terminating his employment. The guard sought a review of the order of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which affirmed the finding of the department's secretary of personnel that the employee's behavior did not promote the efficiency of the public services that the correctional institution performed.


OVERVIEW: The guard allegedly made racially derogatory comments at a bank teller out of anger. The guard was employed on a probationary basis at a correctional institution. The warden terminated his employment upon learning of the situation. After several levels of the guard's appeals, the secretary of personnel found the behavior to have been adverse to the job of correctional officer and a cause of difficulty to the efficiency of the public services performed. The court noted that when a public employee spoke upon matters of public concern not as a citizen but as an employee on matters only of personal interest, absent the most unusual circumstances, such speech would not be given too high a priority in its protection under the First Amendment. The court found the guard's speech to be personal in nature. The guard's overreaction at the bank and resort to ethnic epithets in a peaceful surrounding, triggered by a relatively minor irritant, gave foundation to the department's concern that the guard would resort to similar behavior if frustrated under the considerable pressures of attempting to maintain order at the corrections institution. The court, thus, affirmed the circuit court's order.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the circuit court and ordered that costs would be paid by the guard.

Murphy v. Edmonds


No. 99, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 342; 601 A.2d 102; 1992 Md. LEXIS 23



February 7, 1992, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Baltimore County). Joseph F. Murphy, Jr. JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED. PLAINTIFFS TO PAY COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff injured party challenged the decision of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which reversed the trial court and held that Md. Code. Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 11-108 provided a constitutional cap on non-economic damages in a tort case.


OVERVIEW: Plaintiff injured party filed a lawsuit against defendant company as the result of the injuries the injured party received from a car accident with the company's employee. The jury found in favor of the injured party and awarded substantial damages in excess of the statutory cap provided by Md. Code. Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 11-108 regarding non-economic damages. The trial court found that § 11-108 violated the equal protection guarantee in Md. Const. Decl. Rights art. 24. The court of special appeals reversed and held that the statute was constitutional. On review, the court found that the statute was subject to the rational basis test. The court found that the statutory limitation upon recoverable noneconomic tort damages under Md. Code. Ann., Cts. & Jud. Proc. § 11-108 did not amount to a restriction upon access to the courts. Instead, § 11-108 modified the law of damages to be applied in tort cases. Furthermore, § 11-108 was enacted in response to a legislatively perceived crisis concerning the availability and cost of liability insurance. Therefore, the cap was reasonably related to a legitime legislative objective. The judgment of the court of special appeals was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The appeals court decision that reversed the trial court was affirmed. The statutory cap on non-economic damages was constitution because it was rationally related to a legitimate government interest and did not restrict access to the courts.

State v. Thomas


No. 146, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 160; 599 A.2d 1171; 1992 Md. LEXIS 9



January 9, 1992, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As corrected January 14, 1992.


PRIOR HISTORY: Appeal from the partial grant of post conviction relief in a capital case (Circuit Court for Baltimore County). Joseph F. Murphy, Jr. JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT GRANTING NEW SENTENCING HEARING VACATED; JUDGMENT DENYING POST CONVICTION RELIEF FROM CONVICTIONS AFFIRMED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The state appealed from the order of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which awarded a partial grant of post conviction relief to plaintiff prisoner in a capital case.


OVERVIEW: A jury convicted defendant of two first-degree murders, first-degree rape and robbery. Defendant filed a petition for post conviction relief and the trial court vacated the death sentence and ordered a new sentencing hearing because trial counsel failed to render effective assistance when he permitted defendant to be re-examined by a State psychiatrist following the convictions in preparation for the psychiatrist's testimony at the sentencing hearing. Defendant maintained the actions of his attorney prejudiced him at the sentencing hearing. The state appealed the grant of a new sentencing hearing. On appeal, the court found that the hearing judge erred in excluding testimony from counsel as to what he had learned from a defense expert about defendant because such knowledge was relevant to the reasonableness of counsel's strategy in permitting the post-verdict, pre-sentence interview of defendant by the state's expert. The court vacated the judgment granting partial post conviction relief and remanded the case for admission of that excluded testimony and further consideration, in light of that evidence, of the reasonableness of counsel's decision.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the judgment granting a new sentencing hearing, affirmed the denial of post-conviction relief form convictions and remanded the case for further proceedings because the hearing judge improperly excluded evidence to determine the reasonableness of the strategy of defendant prisoner's attorney.

State Rds. Com. of State Highway Admin. v. 370 Ltd. Partnership


No. 28, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 96; 599 A.2d 449; 1991 Md. LEXIS 211



December 23, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Montgomery County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals. William C. Miller, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART; CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR DETERMINATION AND AWARD OF PREJUDGMENT INTEREST. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE STATE ROADS COMMISSION OF THE STATE HIGHWAY ADMINISTRATION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner state appealed the order of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which valued the condemned property as of the date of trial in the state's "quick take" condemnation action against respondent condemnee under Md. Code Ann., Transp. §§ 8-318 to 8-331 (1977, Cum. Supp. 1991). The condemnee cross-appealed the denial of prejudgment interest and filed a writ of certiorari petition. The state filed a conditional cross-petition.


OVERVIEW: The state utilized the statutory "quick-take" procedures under §§ 8-318 to 8-331 to freeze an early date of valuation and to permit an immediate taking of land. However, the state, inter alia, did not file its formal petition for condemnation within one year of its initial filing. The court affirmed in part and denied in part the trial court's judgment and remanded for a determination and award of prejudgment interest to condemnee. The court held, inter alia, that (1) the trial judge correctly applied a trial date valuation based on the clear language of Md. Code Ann., Transp. § 8-330 (1977, Cum. Supp. 1991) because the state failed to file formal condemnation petitions within one year of the initial "quick-take" petitions; (2) the trial judge was within his discretion to exclude the expert appraiser's testimony proffered by the state as a rebuttal witness because his evidence was cumulative and the condemnee had been unfairly deprived of an opportunity to prepare for his testimony; and (3) the trial court incorrectly declined to award the prejudgment interest mandated by statute and payable from the date of deposit notwithstanding the application of the trial date valuation.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and denied in part the trial court's condemnation judgment, which awarded the condemnee the properties' value based on a trial date valuation, but denied prejudgment interest to the condemnee. The court held that the trial court correctly applied the trial date valuation. However, the court remanded the case to the trial court for a determination and award of prejudgment interest to the condemnee.

Huff v. State


No. 16 September Term, 1991


Court of Appeals of Maryland


325 Md. 55; 599 A.2d 428; 1991 Md. LEXIS 210



December 20, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Circuit Court for Caroline County. J. Owen Wise, JUDGE



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR CAROLINE COUNTY VACATED. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR THE ENTRY OF AN ORDER REMANDING THIS CAUSE TO THE DISTRICT COURT OF MARYLAND SITTING IN CAROLINE COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT TO BE PAID BY THE COUNTY COMMISSIONERS OF CAROLINE COUNTY. COSTS IN THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR CAROLINE COUNTY TO BE DIVIDED BETWEEN PETITIONER, CLINTON R. HUFF, AND THE COUNTY COMMISSIONERS OF CAROLINE COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner sought review of orders of the Circuit Court for Caroline County (Maryland), which denied his motion to dismiss on double jeopardy grounds and denied his motion to dismiss or to remand to the district court. Petitioner also sought review of the circuit court's judgment, which convicted him of homicide, driving while intoxicated, and driving on a revoked license.


OVERVIEW: Petitioner filed a motion to dismiss on double jeopardy grounds, and the district court denied the motion. On appeal, the circuit court denied the motion to dismiss on double jeopardy grounds and set the case for trial. Petitioner filed a written motion to dismiss or to remand to the district court, arguing that only the ruling of the district court on his motion to dismiss on double jeopardy grounds had been appealed to the circuit court. The motion was denied. The circuit court convicted petitioner of homicide, driving while intoxicated, and driving on a revoked license. On appeal, the court held that because the collateral order doctrine did not apply to de novo appeals from the district court, petitioner's appeal was not before the circuit court. Consequently, there was no legal effect to the circuit court's denial of petitioner's motion to dismiss or the circuit court's judgment of conviction. The court remanded the case to the district court for further proceedings on all pending charges. The court noted that petitioner's double jeopardy argument failed because it was a single prosecution for multiple offenses, not a successive prosecution.


OUTCOME: The court vacated the orders and judgment of the circuit court and remanded the case for the entry of an order remanding the case to the district court.

Adkins v. State


No. 26, September Term, 1991


Court of Appeals of Maryland


324 Md. 641; 598 A.2d 194; 1991 Md. LEXIS 200



November 13, 1991  
November 13, 1991, Filed


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected November 21, 1991. As Corrected December 2, 1991.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals; Circuit Court for Baltimore City; John Prevas, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS, CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY MAYOR AND CITY COUNCIL OF BALTIMORE.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner probationer was convicted of violation of probation by respondent State. The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the trial court's judgment. The probationer appealed.


OVERVIEW: The probationer received probation as his sentence for malicious destruction of property, unlawfully carrying a deadly weapon, and battery. The probationer was charged with violation of probation, but he was not served the charges until his probation expired. The probationer was convicted of violation of probation. The lower court dismissed the appeal as moot because the probationer served his sentence while his appeal was pending. The court reversed because there were potential collateral consequences to the conviction. The probationer showed that if the conviction stood, his future job prospects and sentencing for future convictions could have been adversely affected. There was no difference between a defendant having been allowed to challenge a substantive conviction after the sentence was served and the probationer's having challenged his conviction after his sentence was served.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the decision against the probationer in his action to challenge his conviction for violation of probation.

Wiggins v. State


No. 139, September Term, 1989


Court of Appeals of Maryland


324 Md. 551; 597 A.2d 1359; 1991 Md. LEXIS 193



November 8, 1991  
November 8, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal and review of death sentence from the Circuit Court for Baltimore County; J. William Hinkel, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED, EXCEPT AS TO THEFT COUNTS 4 AND 5 OF THE INDICTMENT, AS TO WHICH THE JUDGMENTS ARE VACATED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of the judgment of the Circuit Court for Baltimore County (Maryland), which convicted him of willful, deliberate, and premeditated murder, robbery, and two counts of theft, and sentenced him to death pursuant to the state's death penalty statute, Md. Code Ann. art. 27, § 412(b) (1987 Repl. Vol.).


OVERVIEW: Defendant was convicted of the premeditated murder of an elderly woman. Defendant maintained that he was entitled to a new trial as to his guilt of these offenses because the evidence was insufficient to establish that he was the perpetrator of the crimes and because the trial court erred in denying his motion for a new trial. Defendant also urged, for 12 reasons, that the imposition of the death penalty was improper and a new sentencing hearing was therefore required. The court affirmed the convictions and sentence, except that it vacated the theft convictions as they were lesser included offenses in the robbery conviction. The court rejected defendant's assertion that because the convictions rested solely on circumstantial evidence, they could not be sustained unless they were inconsistent with any reasonable hypothesis of innocence. Rather, the standard applied was whether the record reasonably supported a finding of guilt beyond a reasonable doubt. The court found that defendant could have been sentenced to death because the record revealed that he was present near the victim's apartment and could have, by his own hand, perpetrated the crimes.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the convictions except to vacate the lesser included offenses, the theft convictions.

Ritchie v. Donnelly


No. 132, September Term, 1989


Court of Appeals of Maryland


324 Md. 344; 597 A.2d 432; 1991 Md. LEXIS 183



October 29, 1991  
October 29, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals; Circuit Court for Howard County; Robert F. Fischer, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART, AND CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO AFFIRM THE CIRCUIT COURT'S JUDGMENT IN PART AND REVERSE THAT JUDGMENT IN PART AS INDICATED IN THIS OPINION, AND TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HOWARD COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID ONE-THIRD BY THE PLAINTIFF AND TWO-THIRDS BY THE DEFENDANT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner former deputy sheriff challenged her termination by respondent sheriff, claiming violations of the Maryland Declaration of Rights and of United States Constitution. The trial court dismissed the claims. The appellate court affirmed the dismissal of all federal claims and of the state constitutional claims in respect to "individual capacity" claims, but reversed as to "official capacity" claims. Both parties sought review.


OVERVIEW: Deputy sheriff's federal claims were brought under 42 U.S.C.S. § 1983, alleging violations of the Equal Protection and Due Process Clauses of the Fourteenth Amendment. The sheriff was acting as a state official, and so the sheriff was not a "person" within the meaning of § 1983 with regard to official capacity claims for damages. Therefore, those claims were properly dismissed. However, the lower court erred in dismissing the individual capacity § 1983 claims because the gist of the sheriff's allegedly wrongful conduct was discharging the deputy sheriff because of her sex. While the sheriff clearly acted within the scope of her government employment when she discharged the deputy sheriff, she was not acting pursuant to a policy or custom of the state or county sheriff's office. As to the state constitutional claims, the appellate court erred in splitting the claims into official capacity and individual capacity claims, because state law, contrary to, federal law, does not allow this bifurcation. The dismissal of the state constitutional claims should have been reversed because state officials, unlike the state itself, are not entitled to immunity from compensatory damages.


OUTCOME: The appellate court's judgment was affirmed in part and reversed in part, and the case was remanded with directions to affirm the trial court's judgment in part and reverse that judgment in part, and to remand to the trial court for further proceedings consistent with the court's opinion.

Maryland Pennysaver Group, Inc. v. Comptroller of Treasury


No. 120, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


323 Md. 697; 594 A.2d 1142; 1991 Md. LEXIS 152; 19 Media L. Rep. 1937



September 10, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County pursuant to certiorari to Court of Special Appeals; Bruce C. Williams, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY AFFIRMED. COSTS TO BE PAID BY THE PETITIONER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In a tax refund case, petitioner taxpayer sought review of a judgment of the Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County (Maryland), which found in favor of respondent comptroller and determined that the taxpayer was not entitled to a statutory exemption for newspapers.


OVERVIEW: The taxpayer published shopper's guides and sought a refund for state sales tax it paid. It claimed that it was entitled to the statutory exemption under Md. Ann. Code § 326(n) (1957). The comptroller contended that under its administrative regulations that the shopper guides were not newspapers for the purposes of the statutory exemption. The trial court rendered judgment for the comptroller, and the taxpayer sought review. The court affirmed. It found that the sales tax regulation did not improperly limit the scope of the statutory exemption from sales tax for the printing and sale of newspapers of any and all types. Furthermore, the regulation was not arbitrary and capricious. The court also found that the comptroller's refusal to grant the exemption to the taxpayer did not violate the petitioner's right to free speech under U.S. Const. amend. I and U.S. Const. amend. XIV because the restriction was not more extensive than necessary to serve the governmental interest directly advanced by the restriction.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment for the comptroller and found that the taxpayer was not entitled to the statutory exemption for newspapers.

Briscoe v. Prince George's County Health Dep't


No. 82, September Term, 1989


Court of Appeals of Maryland


323 Md. 439; 593 A.2d 1109; 1991 Md. LEXIS 132

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner employees sought review of a decision of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) that reversed a trial court's decision in their favor in their action against defendants, the Prince George's County Department of Health and the State Department of Health and Mental Hygiene (state). The trial court reversed a decision of the Department of Personnel and ordered the Department of Personnel to reclassify the employees.


OVERVIEW: The employees were clinical social workers employed by the state. They claimed that the state violated the Equal Protection Clause of the Fourteenth Amendment and the state Merit System Law, Md. Ann. Code art. 64A, § 27, by paying them less than psychiatric nurses employed at the same facilities. The employees contended that the state was required to pay comparable salaries to persons in positions involving comparable duties, experience, responsibilities and authority. On appeal, the court agreed with the intermediate appellate court that there was a rational basis for the classification drawn by the state between psychiatric nurses and clinical social workers. The positions of clinical social workers and psychiatric nurses involved different professions with different qualifications and the nurses had the capability to perform functions that the social workers could not perform. Therefore, the classification did not violate the Equal Protection Clause and the decision was affirmed.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the decision of the intermediate appellate court in favor of the state and against the employees in the employees' action to reclassify their positions.

Finci v. American Casualty Co.


No. 81, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


323 Md. 358; 593 A.2d 1069; 1991 Md. LEXIS 125; 21 A.L.R.5th 889



August 16, 1991, Filed


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals; (Circuit Court for Baltimore City); Joseph H. H. Kaplan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASES REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO REMAND THESE CASES TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY STATE OF MARYLAND DEPOSIT INSURANCE FUND CORPORATION.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellants, director and insurers, challenged the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) decision which affirmed summary judgments that awarded appellee receiver all of the proceeds from an insurance policy that appellants alleged did not cover the claims against the directors and to which appellants claimed appellee was not entitled to priority.


OVERVIEW: Appellee receiver sought all of the proceeds from an insurance policy. Appellants, director and insurers, contended that the insurance policy did not cover the claims against the directors. Further, appellants alleged that appellee was not entitled to priority. The court held that the exclusions in the policies relied on by appellee were not ambiguous. The claims of the institution were excluded from coverage. Appellee alleged that public policy prevented the application of the exclusions. The court held that public policy did not prevent the enforcement of the exclusions. The insured versus insured endorsement, which did not bar derivative actions, just actions of one insured against another, was also not invalidated by public policy. Further, appellant director's claims were not moot because appellee did not automatically have priority. The court reversed and remanded the summary judgments.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded, holding that the exclusions in the policies relied on by appellee receiver were not ambiguous, public policy did not prevent the enforcement of the exclusions, public policy did not invalidate the insured versus insured endorsement, and appellant director's claims were not moot.

Jordan v. State


No. 53, September Term, 1990


Court of Appeals of Maryland


323 Md. 151; 591 A.2d 875; 1991 Md. LEXIS 108



June 28, 1991, Filed



DISPOSITION:  [***1] JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART, AND REVERSED IN PART. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH INSTRUCTIONS TO VACATE THE JUDGMENT OF CONVICTION ON CONSPIRACY TO COMMIT ROBBERY, AND TO AFFIRM THE JUDGMENT OF CONVICTION ON THE REMAINING COUNTS. COSTS TO BE DIVIDED EQUALLY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the judgment of the Circuit Court for Carroll County (Maryland), which convicted him of felony murder, conspiracy to commit murder, conspiracy to commit robbery, and robbery, and imposed two life sentences and two 10-year sentences for the respective offenses, with all sentences to run consecutively.


OVERVIEW: Defendant challenged his convictions and sentences. On appeal, the issue was whether the trial court's ruling that defendant's prior statement was voluntary was preserved for appeal where defendant failed to testify on his own behalf and proffered to the court that, but for its ruling, he would have exercised his right to testify. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part. The court stated that defendant's alleged injury was remote and speculative. Assuming defendant was correct, it was not clear how his constitutional rights were violated. His right against self-incrimination was not infringed upon, as he elected not to testify. In order to raise and preserve for review the claim of improper impeachment with a prior conviction, defendant was required to have testified at trial and been impeached. Additionally, the court found that the imposition of one sentence for conspiracy to commit murder and one sentence for conspiracy to commit robbery was plain error, and an illegal sentence resulted. The court noted that only one sentence could have been imposed for a single common law conspiracy no matter how many criminal acts the conspirators had agreed to commit.


OUTCOME: The judgment convicting and sentencing defendant was affirmed in part and reversed in part, as the trial court erred in imposing separate sentences for conspiracy to commit murder and conspiracy to commit robbery. The judgment was remanded with instructions to vacate the conviction for conspiracy to commit robbery.

OVERVIEW: The developer sought to commercial develop an area of the county. The developer obtained the necessary approvals from the county to proceed. In an action by the challenger, the lower court reversed approvals of the developer's project plan and preliminary plan after concluding that certain features of the county's sector plan were invalid. Hunt v. State

Court of Appeals of Maryland


321 Md. 387; 583 A.2d 218; 1990 Md. LEXIS 194

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT AFFIRMED.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant appealed from the order of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which upon a jury verdict sentenced defendant to death for the first degree murder of a police officer after defendant's initial death sentence was vacated by the court.


OVERVIEW: Defendant had shot and killed a police officer who was attempting to arrest him. The court rejected each of defendant's many assertions of error in his resentencing trial. The trial court did not abuse its discretion in ordering defendant shackled upon the indication that defendant was an escape risk. It was not error to refuse to excuse jurors with a stated predisposition to assess the death penalty when they affirmed that they could conscientiously apply the law and find the facts. It was not error to admit testimony and physical evidence reflecting defendant's conduct in prison, his intentions to escape from prison, or expert opinion testimony as to defendant's dangerousness.


OUTCOME: The judgment of the trial court was affirmed.

Schochet v. State


No. 76, September Term, 1988


Court of Appeals of Maryland


320 Md. 714; 580 A.2d 176; 1990 Md. LEXIS 161



October 9, 1990 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Corrected October 16, 1990.


PRIOR HISTORY: Certiorari to Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Montgomery County); Irma S. Raker, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED. CASE REMANDED TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO REVERSE THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY AND REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR MONTGOMERY COUNTY WITH DIRECTIONS THAT A JUDGMENT OF ACQUITTAL BE ENTERED. COSTS IN THIS COURT AND IN THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS TO BE PAID BY MONTGOMERY COUNTY.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) affirmed the defendant's conviction for committing a certain unnatural and perverted sexual practice, fellatio, in violation of Md. Ann. Code of 1957, art. 27, § 554. The petitioner filed an application for a writ of certiorari.


OVERVIEW: The defendant contended that § 554, which prohibited fellatio, did not apply to consensual conduct between two heterosexual adults, in the privacy of one of their homes. The state argued that the applicability of § 554 must be determined from a constitutional basis, and that if private conduct was excepted, the statute would have expressly excluded it. The court construed the statute narrowly and held that the prohibitions of § 554 did not encompass consensual, noncommercial, heterosexual activity between adults in the privacy of the home. The court construed § 554 so as to avoid a serious constitutional question. The court stated that in order to avoid serious constitutional issues, it had repeatedly given a narrow construction to statutes containing broad general language, such as § 554. The statute's silence concerning the matters of consent, privacy, and marriage created legitimate questions regarding its reach. The court observed that the cases that upheld convictions under § 554 involved homosexual activity, minors, or public places, but not private, consensual acts.


OUTCOME: The court reversed defendant's conviction.
