DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY REVERSED. APPELLEE TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: The Circuit Court for Prince George's County (Maryland) entered an order granting appellee commission's motion to amend. Appellant attorney sought review. The court granted certiorari prior to consideration of the case by the intermediate appellate court.


OVERVIEW: The attorney grievance commission received complaints concerning letters mailed to very recently injured persons informing the latter of the availability of the attorney's legal services. Subsequently, an inquiry panel of the bar served the attorney with a certified letter in lieu of subpoena. The letter required the attorney's attendance at a hearing and demanded the production of various documents. In response, the attorney filed a motion for a protective order to quash the subpoena. The attorney appeared at the inquiry panel hearing but refused to answer questions or produce the subpoenaed materials. Consequently, the commission filed a motion for contempt. The circuit court's order directed the attorney to produce certain documents. The commission subsequently filed a motion to amend. After much procedural wrangling, the circuit court granted the motion to amend and compelled the attorney to appear and answer questions before the inquiry panel. On appeal, the court concluded that it was unreasonable to burden the attorney with producing the requested documents or to require him to testify at an inquiry panel hearing.


OUTCOME: The judgment was reversed and the commission was directed to pay the costs.

Eagan v. Ayd


No. 176, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


313 Md. 265; 545 A.2d 55; 1988 Md. LEXIS 105



August 4, 1988 


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 5, 1988.


PRIOR HISTORY: Circuit Court for Harford County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals William O. Carr, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR HARFORD COUNTY AFFIRMED. APPELLANT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellee mother initiated a paternity action in the Circuit Court for Harford County (Maryland) against appellant father alleging that he was the father of her minor daughter. The circuit court held that the father's refusal to submit to a blood test constituted contempt of court. The father appealed.


OVERVIEW: The circuit court ordered the father to submit to a blood test to establish paternity of the mother's minor child. He failed to appear for the test. Eventually, the circuit court issued an order finding him in civil contempt of court for his failure to comply with the blood-test order. The contempt order further provided that he should have submitted to a blood test within five days of the date of the order to purge himself of his contempt or be committed to the jurisdiction of the detention center. The court held that the General Assembly did not mean to preclude the use of the contempt power to compel a reluctant father to submit to the blood test under Md. Code Ann., Fam. Law § 5-1029. Thus, the contempt order by the circuit court was proper.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the circuit court.

State v. Garlick


No. 159, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


313 Md. 209; 545 A.2d 27; 1988 Md. LEXIS 98



August 2, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Anne Arundel County) Eugene M. Lerner, JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO AFFIRM THE JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR ANNE ARUNDEL COUNTY. RESPONDENT TO PAY THE COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Defendant sought review of an order of the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which reversed the circuit court's judgment, holding defendant guilty of driving while under the influence of a controlled dangerous substance and of failure to reduce speed to avoid an accident, and sentenced him to 60 days in prison, along with a fine of $ 300.


OVERVIEW: The driver, traveling at a high rate of speed, swerved from one lane to another and ran into the rear of a car stopped at a toll plaza. In the emergency room, the doctor ordered a toxicological screen of his blood and urine. His blood screen showed the presence of phencyclidine. The driver did not receive a supplementary laboratory technician's report until the day of trial. At trial, the hospital technician was not present, and the report was suppressed. The doctor appeared as a witness and the emergency service report, including the test results, was admitted into evidence. The special appeals court held it was error to admit evidence showing the result of the toxic screen test and reversed the driver's conviction. The court disagreed, noting that the testing was performed in the hospital and not by a police laboratory and no extensive foundation needed to be laid. The court held that the testimony of the emergency room physician showing that the emergency service report was made in the "regular course of business" and that the toxicological screen of the driver's blood and urine was "pathologically germane to treatment" was sufficient to justify admission.


OUTCOME: The court reversed the judgment of the court of special appeals, and remanded the case with directions to affirm the judgment of the circuit court.

O'Leary v. Shipley


No. 138, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


313 Md. 189; 545 A.2d 17; 1988 Md. LEXIS 97; 3 I.E.R. Cas. (BNA) 1517



August 2, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Carroll County pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Eugene M. Lerner, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT REVERSED; CASE REMANDED TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR CARROLL COUNTY FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO ABIDE THE RESULT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff deputy court clerk sought review of a decision by the Circuit Court for Carroll County (Maryland), which entered a judgment in favor of defendant court clerk in the deputy's action, seeking reinstatement and back pay and claiming that the court clerk had wrongfully refused to reappoint her because she opposed him in the election.


OVERVIEW: The deputy opposed the court clerk in an election. The court clerk won re-election whereupon he advised the deputy that he would not reappoint her as a deputy. The deputy brought an action seeking reinstatement and back pay. The lower court found in favor of the court clerk. Upon appeal, the court reversed and remanded, holding that the lower court applied the incorrect test to the facts of the matter. The court found that the deputy was alleging that her overt expressive conduct in challenging the court clerk in the election was considered by the court clerk and played a role, if not the sole role, in his employment decision. Therefore, the lower court should have determined by a balancing test, whether the protected conduct of the deputies campaign was a substantial factor in the court clerk's decision not to reappoint the deputy.


OUTCOME: The court reversed and remanded the judgment which had been entered in favor of the court clerk.

NCR Corp. v. Comptroller of Treasury, Income Tax Div.


No. 87, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


313 Md. 118; 544 A.2d 764; 1988 Md. LEXIS 94


January 7, 1988, Argued  
July 29, 1988, Decided


SUBSEQUENT HISTORY:  [***1] As Amended August 3, 1988. Motion for Reconsideration Denied August 25, 1988.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART. CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY FOR ENTRY OF ORDER REMANDING TO THE TAX COURT FOR PROCEEDINGS CONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS TO BE PAID TWO-THIRDS BY NCR AND ONE-THIRD BY THE COMPTROLLER.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant taxpayer sought review of a judgment from the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland), which upheld a tax court's determination that appellant's operation constituted a unitary business for purposes of apportionment, following various corporate tax assessments made by appellee Comptroller of Treasury against appellant.


OVERVIEW: Appellee Comptroller of Treasury made various corporate tax assessments against appellant taxpayer for the tax years 1972 through 1977. A lower court upheld a tax court's determination that appellant's operation constituted a unitary business for purposes of apportionment. Appellant sought review. The court affirmed in part, and reversed in part, concluding that Md. Code art. 81, § 280A required the inclusion of gross-up in appellant's taxable Maryland income. The court held that the due process clause of U.S. Const. amend. XIV did not preclude Maryland from taxing appellant's gross-up income for the tax year 1976. The court found that the tax court was legally wrong when it concluded that it had to exclude the domestic placement income (DPI) from apportionment merely because the interest payers were independent of appellant. The court held that the DPI was subject to apportionment. The court concluded that appellant should have had an opportunity to develop facts as to the extent of any disproportionality, in light of the principles established in the case.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part a lower court's judgment upholding a tax court's determination that appellant taxpayer's operation constituted a unitary business for purposes of apportionment by appellee Comptroller of Treasury because inclusion of gross-up in appellant's taxable income was required, but the tax court was legally wrong when it concluded that it had to exclude the domestic placement income from apportionment.

Stanley v. State


Nos. 82, 107, September Term, 1987


Court of Appeals of Maryland


313 Md. 50; 542 A.2d 1267; 1988 Md. LEXIS 90



July 1, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Appeal from Certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals (Circuit Court for Prince George's County - Vincent J. Femia, Judge). Appeal from Circuit Court for Baltimore County, pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, William R. Buchanan, Sr., JUDGE.



DISPOSITION: IN STANLEY V. STATE, NO. 82, SEPT. TERM, 1987, CASE REMANDED WITHOUT AFFIRMANCE OR REVERSAL TO THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS WITH DIRECTIONS TO REMAND THE CASE TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR PRINCE GEORGE'S COUNTY WITH DIRECTIONS TO THAT COURT TO CONDUCT A HEARING IN ACCORDANCE WITH THIS OPINION. COSTS IN ALL COURTS TO ABIDE THE RESULT. IN TRICE V. STATE, NO. 107, SEPT. TERM, 1987, CASE REMANDED WITHOUT AFFIRMANCE OR REVERSAL TO THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY WITH DIRECTIONS TO CONDUCT FURTHER PROCEEDINGS IN ACCORDANCE WITH THIS OPINION. THE SENTENCE OF 25 YEARS WITHOUT PAROLE REMAINS IN EFFECT PENDING THIS HEARING. THE CONCURRENT SENTENCES OF THREE YEARS FOR MALICIOUS DESTRUCTION ARE VACATED AND THE CASE IS REMANDED FOR RESENTENCING ALSO PENDING THE OUTCOME OF THIS HEARING. COSTS IN THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE COUNTY AND IN THIS COURT TO ABIDE THE RESULT.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Petitioner and appellant sought certiorari review of their convictions to the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland).


OVERVIEW: Petitioner and appellant sought review of their respective convictions on the basis of alleged equal protection violations by the state's discriminatory use of peremptory challenges to strike jury members. The court remanded in both cases for proceedings consistent with the required application of the Batson analysis. The court explained in each instance, petitioner and appellant were convicted by a substantially white or totally white jury after most or all potential jurors who were black were excluded from jury service by peremptory challenges exercised by the state. The court found each man made a sufficient prima facie showing of purposeful discrimination to justify remand for further inquiry. The court observed once a defendant made a prima facie showing of discrimination, the state was required to articulate a legitimate race neutral explanation for the challenges and such reasonably specific explanation had to be related to the particular case to be tried. The court explained once such reason was submitted, the court was required to decide whether discrimination was ultimately established in view of the facts and context.


OUTCOME: The court remanded petitioner's and appellant's cases for further proceedings to apply the Batson procedures to inquire whether the use of peremptory challenges was discriminatory because in each case a prima facie case of purposeful discrimination was sufficiently shown to require the state to articulate a race neutral reason for striking jurors.

DISPOSITION: JUDGMENTS OF THE COURT OF SPECIAL APPEALS REVERSED IN PART AND AFFIRMED IN PART. NIGHTINGALE) AND TO VACATE THE JUDGMENTS OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR FREDERICK COUNTY UNDER COUNTS I, II, AND IV OF CRIMINAL INFORMATION NO. 7317 AND COUNTS II AND III OF CRIMINAL INFORMATION NO. 7319 (STATE V. CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: In consolidated appeals, defendants challenged judgments from the Court of Special Appeals (Maryland) that determined that separate convictions and sentences for both child abuse and second-, third-, or fourth-degree sexual offense were not improper in cases in which the state relied on and proved the sexual offense to sustain the child abuse conviction.


OVERVIEW: In separate trials in different courts, defendants were convicted on several counts of sex-related offenses and child abuse. At trial, the state relied on and proved the sexual offenses to sustain the child abuse convictions. The appellate court affirmed the conviction. On appeal, the court ruled that it could not be determined with precision from the juries' verdicts whether the convictions of child abuse were sustained by the sexual offense convictions, and that such an ambiguity was to be resolved in favor of defendants. Specifically, the court ruled that it could not tell whether the juries' general verdicts of guilty were based on the use of sexual offenses as lesser included offenses, or elements, of child abuse, or whether the child abuse verdicts were based on other reasons, such as some sort of sexual molestation which the juries thought did not rise to the level of a sexual offense in any degree. The court therefore vacated defendants' convictions on the sex offenses.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgments.

Court of Appeals of Maryland

CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Plaintiff citizens filed a petition for certiorari requesting review of an order from the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which dismissed the claims against defendants mayor, city council, police department and police commissioner, and granted summary judgment to defendant police officer, in the citizens' tort action based on an unlawful search of the citizens' home.


OVERVIEW: The police officer forcibly entered the home of the citizens and searched it pursuant to a search warrant. However, the officer had searched the wrong house; the citizens' house did not entirely match the house described in the affidavit. The citizens' brought an action based on invasion of privacy, defamation, negligence, and violations of Md. Const. Decl. Rts. arts. 24 and 26. The trial court granted the police officer's motion for summary judgment based on qualified immunity, and granted the remaining defendants' motion to dismiss based on immunity. On certiorari, the court affirmed the dismissal of the non-constitutional tort claims against the police officer because the officer did not knowingly or deliberately search the wrong house, and thus, the mistake was not malicious. However, because an official who violated an individual's rights under the Maryland Constitution was not entitled to any immunity, the constitutional issues were remanded. The court affirmed the dismissal of the claims against the remaining defendants' based on immunity.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment in favor of the mayor, city council, police department, and police commissioner. The court affirmed in part and reversed in part the judgment in favor of the police officer, and remanded the case for further proceedings.

American Trucking Assos. v. Goldstein


No. 162, September Term, 1987

312 Md. 583; 541 A.2d 955; 1988 Md. LEXIS 74

PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Baltimore City pursuant to certiorari to the Court of Special Appeals, Joseph H. H. Kaplan, Judge.



DISPOSITION: JUDGMENT OF THE CIRCUIT COURT FOR BALTIMORE CITY AFFIRMED IN PART AND REVERSED IN PART, AND CASE REMANDED TO THAT COURT FOR FURTHER PROCEEDINGS NOT INCONSISTENT WITH THIS OPINION. APPELLEES TO PAY COSTS.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: Appellant motor carrier organization challenged the decision of the Circuit Court for Baltimore City (Maryland), which declared a marker tax imposed under Maryland's Road Tax on Motor Carriers Act, specifically Md. Code Ann. art. 81, § 423(a) (1987), unconstitutional but permitted appellees, state officials, counties, and city, to continue collecting the marker tax for a period of time to avoid a financial hardship.


OVERVIEW: Although the state officials, counties and city conceded that the marker tax imposed under Maryland's Road Tax on Motor Carriers Act, specifically Md. Code Ann. art. 81, § 423(a) (1987), was unconstitutional, they argued that the trial court should not enjoin enforcement for a time to avoid imposing substantial hardship on the state and its political subdivisions. The trial court permitted the continued collection of the marker tax. The court affirmed the trial court's holding that the tax was unconstitutional, but reversed the trial court's decision to refuse to enter an immediate injunction against further enforcement of the tax. In requesting the injunction against future enforcement of the statute, the motor carrier organization did not seek retrospective relief because the operative event was not the formulation of the plan to spend future tax dollars, but was the actual imposition of tax liability. Extraordinary circumstances did not exist to delay the effective date of a decision where the financial impact from the injunction on the state was not especially significant.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the trial court's judgment in part and reversed in part. The court affirmed the trial court's decision that the marker tax under Maryland's Road Tax on Motor Carriers Act was unconstitutional, but held that the trial court erred in refusing to enter an immediate injunction against further enforcement of the tax.

In re Petition for Writ of Prohibition


Misc. No. 27 September Term, 1986


Court of Appeals of Maryland


312 Md. 280; 539 A.2d 664; 1988 Md. LEXIS 44



April 8, 1988 


PRIOR HISTORY:  [***1] Circuit Court for Montgomery County, William C. Miller, Judge.



DISPOSITION: PETITION FOR WRIT OF MANDAMUS OR FOR WRIT OF PROHIBITION DISMISSED. COSTS TO BE DIVIDED EQUALLY BETWEEN THE PARTIES.


CASE SUMMARY:


PROCEDURAL POSTURE: State of Maryland challenged the judgment of the Circuit Court for Montgomery County (Maryland), which granted defendant a new trial, after being found guilty by a jury for attempted robbery with a dangerous and deadly weapon, assault with intent to rob, and simple assault, arguing that the trial court overstepped it authority.


OVERVIEW: Defendant was tried and found guilty by a jury of attempted robbery with a dangerous and deadly weapon, assault with intent to rob, and simple assault. The trial court overruled the jury verdict, believing that the verdict was against the weight and credibility of the evidence, and ordered that defendant be retried. State of Maryland filed a writ of prohibition with the court, arguing that the trial court overstepped its authority to grant defendant a new trial. The court held that it had authority to hear the petition, because appellate courts could issue a writ in its management of the lower courts. The court also determined that the trial court was within its statutory and constitutional right when it weighed the credibility and sufficiency of the evidence and overruled the jury verdict. Finally the court concluded that the trial court's ruling granting defendant a new trial would not be overruled, because the issuance of the writ was to be used in extremely narrow and rare circumstances.


OUTCOME: The court affirmed the judgment of the lower court, and held that the trial court was within its authority when it overruled a jury verdict and granted defendant a new trial, after determining that the weight and sufficiency of the evidence was insufficient to sustain a conviction. The court concluded that it would not issue a writ of prohibition because it was not justified by the trial court's action.
